<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Curiosity</i> (rover)</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">American robotic rover exploring Gale Crater on Mars</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Mars rover. For its spaceflight mission, see <a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory">Mars Science Laboratory</a>. For its events on Mars, see <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Timeline of Mars Science Laboratory">Timeline of Mars Science Laboratory</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

19.75 km (12.27 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msl-where_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-msl-where-12">[12]</a></sup> <br/> <small>as of  10 September 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup></small></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div class="" style="width:100%; text-align:center; border-top:1px #aaa solid">Mars rovers (NASA)</div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">← <i><a href="/wiki/Spirit_(rover)" title="Spirit (rover)">Spirit</a></i> / <i><a href="/wiki/Opportunity_(rover)" title="Opportunity (rover)">Opportunity</a></i></div><div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Mars_2020" title="Mars 2020">Mars 2020</a> →</div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><i><b>Curiosity</b></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Car" title="Car">car</a>-sized <a href="/wiki/Rover_(space_exploration)" title="Rover (space exploration)">rover</a> designed to <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">explore</a> <a href="/wiki/Gale_(crater)" title="Gale (crater)">Gale Crater</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> as part of <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory">Mars Science Laboratory</a> mission (MSL).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-Curiosity_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-Curiosity-3">[3]</a></sup> <i>Curiosity</i> was launched from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral</a> on November 26, 2011, at 15:02 UTC aboard the MSL spacecraft and landed on <a href="/wiki/Aeolis_Palus" title="Aeolis Palus">Aeolis Palus</a> in Gale Crater on Mars on August 6, 2012, 05:17 UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abilleira2013_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abilleira2013-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc20120808_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc20120808-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-youtube1_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-youtube1-13">[13]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bradbury_Landing" title="Bradbury Landing">Bradbury Landing</a> site was less than 2.4 km (1.5 mi) from the center of the rover's touchdown target after a 560 million km (350 million mi) journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20120822_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20120822-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-14">[14]</a></sup> The rover's <a href="#Goals_and_objectives">goals</a> include an investigation of the Martian <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Mars" title="Climate of Mars">climate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mars" title="Geology of Mars">geology</a>; assessment of whether the selected field site inside Gale Crater has ever offered <a href="/wiki/Environmental_science" title="Environmental science">environmental conditions</a> favorable for <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars" title="Life on Mars">microbial life</a>, including investigation of the <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">role of water</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">planetary habitability</a> studies in preparation for human exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overview_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-overview-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goals_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-goals-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2012, <i>Curiosity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> two-year mission was extended indefinitely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3news.nz_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-3news.nz-17">[17]</a></sup> On August 5, 2017, NASA celebrated the fifth anniversary of the <i>Curiosity</i> rover landing and related exploratory accomplishments on the planet <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20170802_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20170802-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP-20170805_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-SP-20170805-19">[19]</a></sup> The rover is still operational, and as of October 13, 2018, <i>Curiosity</i> has been on Mars for 2199 <a href="/wiki/Timekeeping_on_Mars#Sols" title="Timekeeping on Mars">sols</a> (2259 <a href="/wiki/Day" title="Day">total days</a>) since landing on August 6, 2012. <i>(See <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Mars_Science_Laboratory#Current_status" title="Timeline of Mars Science Laboratory">current status</a>.)</i>
</p><p><i>Curiosity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> design will serve as the basis for the planned <a href="/wiki/Mars_2020" title="Mars 2020">Mars 2020</a> rover.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Goals_and_objectives">Goals and objectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Goals and objectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As established by the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Program" title="Mars Exploration Program">Mars Exploration Program</a>, the main scientific goals of the MSL mission are to help determine whether Mars could ever have supported <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars" title="Life on Mars">life</a>, as well as determining the <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">role of water</a>, and to study the <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Mars" title="Climate of Mars">climate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mars" title="Geology of Mars">geology of Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overview_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-overview-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goals_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-goals-16">[16]</a></sup> The mission will also help prepare for human exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goals_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-goals-16">[16]</a></sup> To contribute to these goals, MSL has eight main scientific objectives:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.obj_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.obj-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Biological</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<ol start="1" type="1">
<li>Determine the nature and inventory of <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic carbon compounds</a></li>
<li>Investigate the chemical <a href="/wiki/CHON" title="CHON">building blocks of life</a> (carbon, hydrogen, nitrogen, oxygen, phosphorus, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>)</li>
<li>Identify features that may represent the effects of biological processes (<a href="/wiki/Biosignature" title="Biosignature">biosignatures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a>)</li>
</ol>
<dl><dt>Geological and geochemical</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<ol start="4" type="1">
<li>Investigate the chemical, <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopic</a>, and mineralogical composition of the Martian surface and near-surface geological materials</li>
<li>Interpret the processes that have formed and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedology_(soil_study)" title="Pedology (soil study)">modified rocks and soils</a></li>
</ol>
<dl><dt>Planetary process</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<ol start="6" type="1">
<li>Assess long-timescale (i.e., 4-billion-year) <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Martian atmospheric</a> evolution processes</li>
<li>Determine present state, distribution, and <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">cycling of water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a></li>
</ol>
<dl><dt>Surface radiation</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<ol start="8" type="1">
<li>Characterize the broad spectrum of surface radiation, including <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">galactic and cosmic radiation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_proton_event" title="Solar proton event">solar proton events</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron#High-energy_neutrons" title="Neutron">secondary neutrons</a>. As part of its exploration, it also measured the radiation exposure in the interior of the spacecraft as it traveled to Mars, and it is continuing radiation measurements as it explores the surface of Mars. This data would be important for a future <a href="/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars" title="Human mission to Mars">crewed mission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-double_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-double-21">[21]</a></sup>
</li>
</ol>
<p>About one year into the surface mission, and having assessed that ancient Mars could have been hospitable to microbial life, the MSL mission objectives evolved to developing predictive models for the preservation process of <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomolecules" title="Biomolecules">biomolecules</a>; a branch of paleontology called <a href="/wiki/Taphonomy" title="Taphonomy">taphonomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_01-24-2014_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_01-24-2014-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifications">Specifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Specifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Curiosity</i> comprised 23 percent of the mass of the 3,893 kg (8,583 lb) <i><a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory">Mars Science Laboratory</a></i> (MSL) spacecraft, which had the sole mission of delivering the rover safely across space from Earth to a <a href="/wiki/Soft_landing_(rocketry)" title="Soft landing (rocketry)">soft landing</a> on the surface of Mars. The remaining mass of the MSL craft was discarded in the process of carrying out this task.
</p>
<ul><li><b>Dimensions</b>: <i>Curiosity</i> has a mass of 899 kg (1,982 lb) including 80 kg (180 lb) of scientific instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLUSAToday_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSLUSAToday-23">[23]</a></sup> The rover is 2.9 m (9.5 ft) long by 2.7 m (8.9 ft) wide by 2.2 m (7.2 ft) in height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnes_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnes-24">[24]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg/220px-Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg/330px-Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg/440px-Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fueling_of_the_MSL_MMRTG_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Radioisotope within a graphite shell that goes into the generator</div></div></div>
<ul><li><b>Power source</b>: <i>Curiosity</i> is powered by a <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generator</a> (RTG), like the successful <i><a href="/wiki/Viking_1" title="Viking 1">Viking 1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Viking_2" title="Viking 2">Viking 2</a></i> Mars landers in 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMRTG_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMRTG-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarsExplorationMMRTG_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarsExplorationMMRTG-26">[26]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd>Radioisotope power systems (RPSs) are generators that produce electricity from the decay of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive_isotopes" title="Radioactive isotopes">radioactive isotopes</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a>, which is a non-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fissile" title="Fissile">fissile</a> isotope of plutonium. Heat given off by the decay of this isotope is converted into electric voltage by <a href="/wiki/Thermocouple" title="Thermocouple">thermocouples</a>, providing constant power during all seasons and through the day and night. <a href="/wiki/Waste_heat" title="Waste heat">Waste heat</a> can be used via pipes to warm systems, freeing electrical power for the operation of the vehicle and instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMRTG_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-MMRTG-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarsExplorationMMRTG_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-MarsExplorationMMRTG-26">[26]</a></sup> <i>Curiosity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> RTG is fueled by 4.8 kg (11 lb) of <a href="/wiki/Plutonium(IV)_oxide" title="Plutonium(IV) oxide">plutonium-238 dioxide</a> supplied by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Energy" title="U.S. Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sfnow20111117_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-sfnow20111117-27">[27]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><i>Curiosity'</i>s RTG is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-Mission_Radioisotope_Thermoelectric_Generator" title="Multi-Mission Radioisotope Thermoelectric Generator">Multi-Mission Radioisotope Thermoelectric Generator</a> (MMRTG), designed and built by <a href="/wiki/Rocketdyne" title="Rocketdyne">Rocketdyne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teledyne_Technologies" title="Teledyne Technologies">Teledyne Energy Systems</a> under contract to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritz2004_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ritz2004-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cataldo2011_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cataldo2011-29">[29]</a></sup> and assembled and tested by the <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory" title="Idaho National Laboratory">Idaho National Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Idaho2011_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Idaho2011-30">[30]</a></sup> Based on legacy RTG technology, it represents a more flexible and compact development step,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLPower_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSLPower-31">[31]</a></sup> and is designed to produce 110 watts of electrical power and about 2,000 watts of thermal power at the start of the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMRTG_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-MMRTG-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarsExplorationMMRTG_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-MarsExplorationMMRTG-26">[26]</a></sup> The MMRTG produces less power over time as its plutonium fuel decays: at its minimum lifetime of 14 years, electrical power output is down to 100 watts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars_Science_Laboratory_–_Technologies_of_Broad_Benefit:_Power_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_Science_Laboratory_–_Technologies_of_Broad_Benefit:_Power-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overview_of_NASA_Program_on_Development_of_Radioisotope_Power_Systems_with_High_Specific_Power_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Overview_of_NASA_Program_on_Development_of_Radioisotope_Power_Systems_with_High_Specific_Power-33">[33]</a></sup> The power source will generate 9 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megajoule" title="Megajoule">MJ</a> (2.5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watt-hour" title="Watt-hour">kWh</a>) each day, much more than the solar panels of the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover" title="Mars Exploration Rover">Mars Exploration Rovers</a>, which can generate about 2.1 MJ (0.58 kWh) each day. The electrical output from the MMRTG charges two rechargeable <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion batteries</a>. This enables the power subsystem to meet peak power demands of rover activities when the demand temporarily exceeds the generator’s steady output level. Each battery has a capacity of about 42 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ampere-hour" title="Ampere-hour">ampere-hours</a>.</dd></dl>
<ul><li><b>Heat rejection system</b>: The temperatures at the landing site can vary from −127 to 40 °C (−197 to 104 °F); therefore, the thermal system will warm the rover for most of the Martian year. The thermal system will do so in several ways: passively, through the dissipation to internal components; by electrical heaters strategically placed on key components; and by using the rover heat rejection system (HRS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DESCANSO_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-DESCANSO-34">[34]</a></sup> It uses fluid pumped through 60 m (200 ft) of tubing in the rover body so that sensitive components are kept at optimal temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HRS_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-HRS-35">[35]</a></sup> The fluid loop serves the additional purpose of rejecting heat when the rover has become too warm, and it can also gather waste heat from the power source by pumping fluid through two heat exchangers that are mounted alongside the RTG. The HRS also has the ability to cool components if necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HRS_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-HRS-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Computers</b>: The two identical on-board rover computers, called Rover Computer Element (RCE) contain <a href="/wiki/Radiation_hardening" title="Radiation hardening">radiation hardened</a> memory to tolerate the extreme radiation from space and to safeguard against power-off cycles. The computers run the <a href="/wiki/VxWorks" title="VxWorks">VxWorks</a> real-time operating system (RTOS). Each computer's memory includes 256 <a href="/wiki/Kilobyte" title="Kilobyte">kB</a> of <a href="/wiki/EEPROM" title="EEPROM">EEPROM</a>, 256 <a href="/wiki/Megabyte" title="Megabyte">MB</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_random-access_memory" title="Dynamic random-access memory">DRAM</a>, and 2 <a href="/wiki/Gigabyte" title="Gigabyte">GB</a> of <a href="/wiki/Flash_memory" title="Flash memory">flash memory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brains_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brains-36">[36]</a></sup> For comparison, the Mars Exploration Rovers used 3 MB of EEPROM, 128 MB of DRAM, and 256 MB of flash memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieeecomputer_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-ieeecomputer-37">[37]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd>The RCE computers use the <a href="/wiki/RAD750" title="RAD750">RAD750</a> <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">CPU</a>, which is a successor to the <a href="/wiki/IBM_RAD6000" title="IBM RAD6000">RAD6000</a> CPU of the Mars Exploration Rovers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAE_Systems_Computers_to_Manage_Data_Processing_and_Command_For_Upcoming_Satellite_Missions_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-BAE_Systems_Computers_to_Manage_Data_Processing_and_Command_For_Upcoming_Satellite_Missions-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E&amp;ISNow_—_Media_gets_closer_look_at_Manassas_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-E&amp;ISNow_—_Media_gets_closer_look_at_Manassas-39">[39]</a></sup> The RAD750 CPU, a radiation-hardened version of the <a href="/wiki/PowerPC_7xx#PowerPC_740/750" title="PowerPC 7xx">PowerPC 750</a>, can execute up to 400 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Million_instructions_per_second" title="Million instructions per second">MIPS</a>, while the RAD6000 CPU is capable of up to only 35 MIPS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAD750brochure_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-RAD750brochure-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAD6000brochure_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-RAD6000brochure-41">[41]</a></sup> Of the two on-board computers, one is configured as backup and will take over in the event of problems with the main computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brains_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brains-36">[36]</a></sup> On February 28, 2013, NASA was forced to switch to the backup computer due to an issue with the then active computer's flash memory, which resulted in the computer continuously rebooting in a loop. The backup computer was turned on in <a href="/wiki/Safe_mode" title="Safe mode">safe mode</a> and subsequently returned to active status on March 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20130304_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20130304-42">[42]</a></sup> The same issue happened in late March, resuming full operations on March 25, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20130325_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20130325-43">[43]</a></sup></dd>
<dd>The rover has an <a href="/wiki/Inertial_measurement_unit" title="Inertial measurement unit">inertial measurement unit</a> (IMU) that provides 3-axis information on its position, which is used in rover navigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brains_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brains-36">[36]</a></sup> The rover's computers are constantly self-monitoring to keep the rover operational, such as by regulating the rover's temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brains_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Brains-36">[36]</a></sup> Activities such as taking pictures, driving, and operating the instruments are performed in a command sequence that is sent from the flight team to the rover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brains_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Brains-36">[36]</a></sup> The rover installed its full surface operations software after the landing because its computers did not have sufficient main memory available during flight. The new software essentially replaced the flight software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-14">[14]</a></sup></dd>
<dd>The rover has four processors. One of them is a <a href="/wiki/SPARC" title="SPARC">SPARC</a> processor that ran the rover's thrusters and descent-stage motors as it descended through the Martian atmosphere. Two others are <a href="/wiki/PowerPC" title="PowerPC">PowerPC</a> processors: the main processor, which handles nearly all of the rover's ground functions, and that processor's backup. The fourth one, another <a href="/wiki/SPARC" title="SPARC">SPARC</a> processor, commands the rover's movement and is part of its <a href="/wiki/Motor_controller" title="Motor controller">motor controller</a> box. All four processors are <a href="/wiki/Single-core" title="Single-core">single core</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaudin_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaudin-44">[44]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA16106_-_Curiosity_speaks_and_orbiters_listen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="604" data-file-width="896" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/PIA16106_-_Curiosity_speaks_and_orbiters_listen.jpg/350px-PIA16106_-_Curiosity_speaks_and_orbiters_listen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/PIA16106_-_Curiosity_speaks_and_orbiters_listen.jpg/525px-PIA16106_-_Curiosity_speaks_and_orbiters_listen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/PIA16106_-_Curiosity_speaks_and_orbiters_listen.jpg/700px-PIA16106_-_Curiosity_speaks_and_orbiters_listen.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA16106_-_Curiosity_speaks_and_orbiters_listen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Curiosity</i> transmits to Earth directly or via three relay satellites in Mars orbit.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><b>Communications</b>: <i>Curiosity</i> is equipped with significant telecommunication redundancy by several means – an <a href="/wiki/X_band" title="X band">X band</a> <a href="/wiki/Transponder_(satellite_communications)" title="Transponder (satellite communications)">transmitter and receiver</a> that can communicate directly with Earth, and a UHF <a href="/wiki/Electra_(radio)" title="Electra (radio)">Electra</a>-Lite <a href="/wiki/Software-defined_radio" title="Software-defined radio">software-defined radio</a> for communicating with Mars orbiters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DESCANSO_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-DESCANSO-34">[34]</a></sup> Communication with orbiters is the main path for data return to Earth since the orbiters have both more power and larger antennas than the lander allowing for faster transmission speeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DESCANSO_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-DESCANSO-34">[34]</a></sup> Telecommunication includes a small deep space transponder on the descent stage and a solid-state power amplifier on the rover for <a href="/wiki/X_band" title="X band">X band</a>. The rover also has two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UHF" title="UHF">UHF</a> radios,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DESCANSO_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-DESCANSO-34">[34]</a></sup> the signals of which the <a href="/wiki/2001_Mars_Odyssey" title="2001 Mars Odyssey">2001 Mars Odyssey</a> satellite is capable of relaying back to Earth. An average of 14 minutes, 6 seconds will be required for signals to travel between Earth and Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars-Earth_distance_in_light_minutes_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mars-Earth_distance_in_light_minutes-45">[45]</a></sup> <i>Curiosity</i> can communicate with Earth directly at speeds up to 32  kbit/s, but the bulk of the data transfer should be relayed through the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/2001_Mars_Odyssey" title="2001 Mars Odyssey">Odyssey orbiter</a>. Data transfer speeds between <i>Curiosity</i> and each orbiter may reach 2000  kbit/s and 256  kbit/s, respectively, but each orbiter is able to communicate with <i>Curiosity</i> for only about eight minutes per day (0.56% of the time).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curiosity's_data_communication_with_Earth_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curiosity's_data_communication_with_Earth-46">[46]</a></sup> Communication from and to <i>Curiosity</i> relies on internationally agreed space data <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communications_protocol" title="Communications protocol">communications protocols</a> as defined by the Consultative Committee for Space Data Systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aiaa.13350_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-aiaa.13350-47">[47]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JPL" title="JPL">JPL</a> is the central data distribution hub where selected data products are provided to remote science operations sites as needed. JPL is also the central hub for the uplink process, though participants are distributed at their respective home institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DESCANSO_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-DESCANSO-34">[34]</a></sup> At landing, telemetry was monitored by three orbiters, depending on their dynamic location: the <a href="/wiki/2001_Mars_Odyssey" title="2001 Mars Odyssey">2001 Mars Odyssey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter</a> and ESA's <a href="/wiki/Mars_Express" title="Mars Express">Mars Express</a> satellite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA_spacecraft_records_crucial_NASA_signals_from_Mars_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA_spacecraft_records_crucial_NASA_signals_from_Mars-48">[48]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<ul><li><b>Mobility systems</b>: <i>Curiosity</i> is equipped with six 50 cm (20 in) diameter wheels in a <a href="/wiki/Rocker-bogie" title="Rocker-bogie">rocker-bogie</a> suspension. The suspension system also served as landing gear for the vehicle, unlike it's smaller predecessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new_wheels_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-new_wheels-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA.build_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA.build-50">[50]</a></sup> Each wheel has cleats and is independently actuated and geared, providing for climbing in soft sand and scrambling over rocks. Each front and rear wheel can be independently steered, allowing the vehicle to turn in place as well as execute arcing turns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DESCANSO_34-5"><a href="#cite_note-DESCANSO-34">[34]</a></sup> Each wheel has a pattern that helps it maintain traction but also leaves patterned tracks in the sandy surface of Mars. That pattern is used by onboard cameras to estimate the distance traveled. The pattern itself is <a href="/wiki/Morse_code" title="Morse code">Morse code</a> for "JPL" (·--- ·--· ·-··).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-remorse_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-remorse-51">[51]</a></sup> The rover is capable of climbing sand dunes with slopes up to 12.5°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA.249_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA.249-52">[52]</a></sup> Based on the center of mass, the vehicle can withstand a tilt of at least 50° in any direction without overturning, but automatic sensors will limit the rover from exceeding 30° tilts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DESCANSO_34-6"><a href="#cite_note-DESCANSO-34">[34]</a></sup> After two years of use, the wheels are visibly worn with punctures and tears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary20140819_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetary20140819-53">[53]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><i>Curiosity</i> can roll over obstacles approaching 65 cm (26 in) in height,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-facts_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-facts-54">[54]</a></sup> and it has a ground clearance of 60 cm (24 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-first_drive_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-first_drive-55">[55]</a></sup> Based on variables including power levels, terrain difficulty, slippage and visibility, the maximum terrain-traverse speed is estimated to be 200 m (660 ft) per day by automatic navigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-facts_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-facts-54">[54]</a></sup> The rover landed about 10 km (6.2 mi) from the base of Mount Sharp,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stuff-7437621_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-stuff-7437621-56">[56]</a></sup> (officially named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeolis_Mons" title="Aeolis Mons">Aeolis Mons</a>) and it is expected to traverse a minimum of 19 km (12 mi) during its primary two-year mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-home_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-home-57">[57]</a></sup> It can travel up to 90 m (300 ft) per hour but average speed is about 30 m (98 ft) per hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-home_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-home-57">[57]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Instruments">Instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drawing-of-the-Mars-Science_Laboratory.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="465" height="292" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Drawing-of-the-Mars-Science_Laboratory.png/350px-Drawing-of-the-Mars-Science_Laboratory.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Drawing-of-the-Mars-Science_Laboratory.png 1.5x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Drawing-of-the-Mars-Science_Laboratory.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Instrument location diagram</div></div></div>
<p>The general sample analysis strategy begins with high-resolution cameras to look for features of interest. If a particular surface is of interest, <i>Curiosity</i> can vaporize a small portion of it with an infrared laser and examine the resulting spectra signature to query the rock's elemental composition. If that signature is intriguing, the rover will use its long arm to swing over a <a href="/wiki/Mars_Hand_Lens_Imager" title="Mars Hand Lens Imager">microscope</a> and an <a href="/wiki/X-ray_spectroscopy" title="X-ray spectroscopy">X-ray spectrometer</a> to take a closer look. If the specimen warrants further analysis, <i>Curiosity</i> can drill into the boulder and deliver a powdered sample to either the <a href="/wiki/Sample_Analysis_at_Mars" title="Sample Analysis at Mars">SAM</a> or the <a href="/wiki/CheMin" title="CheMin">CheMin</a> analytical laboratories inside the rover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gale_Crater:_Geological_'sweet_shop'_awaits_Mars_rover_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gale_Crater:_Geological_'sweet_shop'_awaits_Mars_rover-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLSAM_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSLSAM-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa2_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa2-60">[60]</a></sup> The MastCam, Mars Hand Lens Imager (MAHLI), and Mars Descent Imager (MARDI) cameras were developed by <a href="/wiki/Malin_Space_Science_Systems" title="Malin Space Science Systems">Malin Space Science Systems</a> and they all share common design components, such as on-board electronic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Image_processing" title="Image processing">imaging processing</a> boxes, 1600×1200 <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCDs</a>, and an <a href="/wiki/Bayer_filter" title="Bayer filter">RGB Bayer pattern filter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LPSCMast_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-LPSCMast-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MastCam_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-MastCam-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAHLI_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-MAHLI-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MARDI_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-MARDI-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MastCamDescription_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-MastCamDescription-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NovEmail_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-NovEmail-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has 17 cameras: HazCams (8), NavCams (4), MastCams (2), MAHLI (1), MARDI (1), and ChemCam (1).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired20120807_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-wired20120807-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Mast_Camera_.28MastCam.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mast_Camera_(MastCam)">Mast Camera (MastCam)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Mast Camera (MastCam)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Msl-arm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3952" data-file-width="3938" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Msl-arm.jpg/220px-Msl-arm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Msl-arm.jpg/330px-Msl-arm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Msl-arm.jpg/440px-Msl-arm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Msl-arm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The turret at the end of the robotic arm holds five devices.</div></div></div>
<p>The MastCam system provides multiple spectra and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/True-color" title="True-color">true-color</a> imaging with two cameras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MastCam_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-MastCam-62">[62]</a></sup> The cameras can take true-color images at 1600×1200 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pixels" title="Pixels">pixels</a> and up to 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frames_per_second" title="Frames per second">frames per second</a> hardware-compressed video at <a href="/wiki/720p" title="720p">720p</a> (1280×720).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arstech20120807_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-arstech20120807-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>One MastCam camera is the Medium Angle Camera (MAC), which has a 34 mm (1.3 in) <a href="/wiki/Focal_length" title="Focal length">focal length</a>, a 15° <a href="/wiki/Field_of_view" title="Field of view">field of view</a>, and can yield 22 cm/pixel (8.7 in/pixel) scale at 1 km (0.62 mi). The other camera in the MastCam is the Narrow Angle Camera (NAC), which has a 100 mm (3.9 in) focal length, a 5.1° field of view, and can yield 7.4 cm/pixel (2.9 in/pixel) scale at 1 km (0.62 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MastCam_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-MastCam-62">[62]</a></sup> Malin also developed a pair of MastCams with zoom lenses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars_Science_Laboratory_(MSL)_Mast_Camera_(MastCam)_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_Science_Laboratory_(MSL)_Mast_Camera_(MastCam)-69">[69]</a></sup> but these were not included in the rover because of the time required to test the new hardware and the looming November 2011 launch date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Nixes_3-D_Camera_for_Next_Mars_Rover_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Nixes_3-D_Camera_for_Next_Mars_Rover-70">[70]</a></sup> However, the improved zoom version was selected to be incorporated on the upcoming <a href="/wiki/Mars_2020" title="Mars 2020">Mars 2020</a> mission as <a href="/wiki/Mastcam-Z" title="Mastcam-Z">Mastcam-Z</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell2014a_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bell2014a-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Each camera has eight gigabytes of flash memory, which is capable of storing over 5,500 raw images, and can apply real time <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lossless_data_compression" title="Lossless data compression">lossless data compression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MastCam_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-MastCam-62">[62]</a></sup> The cameras have an autofocus capability that allows them to focus on objects from 2.1 m (6 ft 11 in) to infinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MastCamDescription_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-MastCamDescription-65">[65]</a></sup> In addition to the fixed <b>RGBG</b> <a href="/wiki/Bayer_filter" title="Bayer filter">Bayer pattern filter</a>, each camera has an eight-position filter wheel. While the Bayer filter reduces visible light throughput, all three colors are mostly transparent at wavelengths longer than 700 nm, and have minimal effect on such <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MastCam_62-4"><a href="#cite_note-MastCam-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span id="Chemistry_and_Camera_complex_.28ChemCam.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry_and_Camera_complex_(ChemCam)">Chemistry and Camera complex (ChemCam)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Chemistry and Camera complex (ChemCam)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chemistry_and_Camera_complex" title="Chemistry and Camera complex">Chemistry and Camera complex</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:483646main_pia13398-4x3_1600-1200.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/483646main_pia13398-4x3_1600-1200.jpg/220px-483646main_pia13398-4x3_1600-1200.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/483646main_pia13398-4x3_1600-1200.jpg/330px-483646main_pia13398-4x3_1600-1200.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/483646main_pia13398-4x3_1600-1200.jpg/440px-483646main_pia13398-4x3_1600-1200.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:483646main_pia13398-4x3_1600-1200.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The internal spectrometer (left) and the laser telescope (right) for the mast</div></div></div>
<p>ChemCam is a suite of remote sensing instruments, and as the name implies, ChemCam is actually two different instruments combined as one: a <a href="/wiki/Laser-induced_breakdown_spectroscopy" title="Laser-induced breakdown spectroscopy">laser-induced breakdown spectroscopy</a> (LIBS) and a Remote Micro Imager (RMI) telescope. The ChemCam instrument suite was developed by the French <a href="/wiki/Centre_d%27Etude_Spatiale_des_Rayonnements" title="Centre d'Etude Spatiale des Rayonnements">CESR</a> laboratory and the <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLChemCam_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSLChemCam-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comparative_study_of_different_methodologies_for_quantitative_rock_analysis_by_Laser-Induced_Breakdown_Spectroscopy_in_a_simulated_Martian_atmosphere_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Comparative_study_of_different_methodologies_for_quantitative_rock_analysis_by_Laser-Induced_Breakdown_Spectroscopy_in_a_simulated_Martian_atmosphere-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corrections_and_Clarifications,_News_of_the_Week_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corrections_and_Clarifications,_News_of_the_Week-74">[74]</a></sup> The flight model of the mast unit was delivered from the French <a href="/wiki/CNES" title="CNES">CNES</a> to <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos National Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanl_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-75">[75]</a></sup> The purpose of the LIBS instrument is to provide elemental compositions of rock and soil, while the RMI will give ChemCam scientists high-resolution images of the sampling areas of the rocks and soil that LIBS targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLChemCam_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-MSLChemCam-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spacecraft:_Surface_Operations_Configuration:_Science_Instruments:_ChemCam_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spacecraft:_Surface_Operations_Configuration:_Science_Instruments:_ChemCam-76">[76]</a></sup> The LIBS instrument can target a rock or soil sample up to 7 m (23 ft) away, vaporizing a small amount of it with about 50 to 75 5-nanosecond pulses from a 1067 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a> <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> laser and then observing the spectrum of the light emitted by the vaporized rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-softpedia20131206_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-softpedia20131206-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA16089.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="774" data-file-width="1826" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA16089.jpg/220px-PIA16089.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA16089.jpg/330px-PIA16089.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA16089.jpg/440px-PIA16089.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA16089.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First <a href="/wiki/Laser-induced_breakdown_spectroscopy" title="Laser-induced breakdown spectroscopy">laser spectrum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> from ChemCam on <i>Curiosity</i> (<a href="/wiki/N165" title="N165">"Coronation" rock</a>, August 19, 2012)</div></div></div>
<p>ChemCam has the ability to record up to 6,144 different wavelengths of ultraviolet, visible, and infrared light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.1315_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.1315-78">[78]</a></sup> Detection of the ball of luminous plasma will be done in the visible, near-UV and near-infrared ranges, between 240 nm and 800 nm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLChemCam_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-MSLChemCam-72">[72]</a></sup> The first initial laser testing of the ChemCam by <i>Curiosity</i> on Mars was performed on a rock, <a href="/wiki/N165" title="N165">N165 ("Coronation" rock)</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Bradbury_Landing" title="Bradbury Landing">Bradbury Landing</a> on August 19, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20120819a_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20120819a-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20120819_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20120819-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20120817_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20120817-81">[81]</a></sup> The ChemCam team expects to take approximately one dozen compositional measurements of rocks per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chemcam_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-chemcam-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Using the same collection optics, the RMI provides context images of the LIBS analysis spots. The RMI resolves 1 mm (0.039 in) objects at 10 m (33 ft) distance, and has a field of view covering 20 cm (7.9 in) at that distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLChemCam_72-3"><a href="#cite_note-MSLChemCam-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Navigation_cameras_.28navcams.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Navigation_cameras_(navcams)">Navigation cameras (navcams)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Navigation cameras (navcams)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_two_full-resolution_images_from_the_Curosity_rover.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="983" data-file-width="1775" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/First_two_full-resolution_images_from_the_Curosity_rover.jpg/220px-First_two_full-resolution_images_from_the_Curosity_rover.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/First_two_full-resolution_images_from_the_Curosity_rover.jpg/330px-First_two_full-resolution_images_from_the_Curosity_rover.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/First_two_full-resolution_images_from_the_Curosity_rover.jpg/440px-First_two_full-resolution_images_from_the_Curosity_rover.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_two_full-resolution_images_from_the_Curosity_rover.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First full-resolution navcam images</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Navcam" title="Navcam">Navcam</a></div>
<p>The rover has two pairs of black and white <a href="/wiki/Navcam" title="Navcam">navigation cameras</a> mounted on the mast to support ground navigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLPhotosynth_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSLPhotosynth-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NavCameras_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-NavCameras-84">[84]</a></sup> The cameras have a 45° <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_view" title="Angle of view">angle of view</a> and use visible light to capture <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopy" title="Stereoscopy">stereoscopic 3-D imagery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NavCameras_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-NavCameras-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary.2012_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetary.2012-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Rover_Environmental_Monitoring_Station_.28REMS.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rover_Environmental_Monitoring_Station_(REMS)">Rover Environmental Monitoring Station (REMS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Rover Environmental Monitoring Station (REMS)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rover_Environmental_Monitoring_Station" title="Rover Environmental Monitoring Station">Rover Environmental Monitoring Station</a></div>
<p>REMS comprises instruments to measure the Mars environment: humidity, pressure, temperatures, wind speeds, and ultraviolet radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rover_Environmental_Monitoring_Station_for_MSL_mission_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rover_Environmental_Monitoring_Station_for_MSL_mission-86">[86]</a></sup> It is a meteorological package that includes an <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> sensor provided by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education_(Spain)" title="Ministry of Education (Spain)">Spanish Ministry of Education and Science</a>. The investigative team is led by Javier Gómez-Elvira of the Center for Astrobiology (Madrid) and includes the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Meteorological_Institute" title="Finnish Meteorological Institute">Finnish Meteorological Institute</a> as a partner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSL_REMS_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSL_REMS-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSL_REMS-pdf_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSL_REMS-pdf-88">[88]</a></sup> All sensors are located around three elements: two booms attached to the rover's mast, the Ultraviolet Sensor (UVS) assembly located on the rover top deck, and the Instrument Control Unit (ICU) inside the rover body. REMS will provide new clues about the Martian general circulation, micro scale weather systems, local hydrological cycle, destructive potential of UV radiation, and subsurface habitability based on ground-atmosphere interaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSL_REMS_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-MSL_REMS-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Hazard_avoidance_cameras_.28Hazcam.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hazard_avoidance_cameras_(Hazcam)">Hazard avoidance cameras (Hazcam)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Hazard avoidance cameras (Hazcam)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hazcam" title="Hazcam">Hazcam</a></div>
<p>The rover has four pairs of black and white navigation cameras called <a href="/wiki/Hazcam" title="Hazcam">hazcams</a>, two pairs in the front and two pairs in the back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLPhotosynth_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-MSLPhotosynth-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HazardAvoidanceCameras_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-HazardAvoidanceCameras-89">[89]</a></sup> They are used for autonomous hazard avoidance during rover drives and for safe positioning of the robotic arm on rocks and soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HazardAvoidanceCameras_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-HazardAvoidanceCameras-89">[89]</a></sup> Each camera in a pair is hardlinked to one of two identical main computers for redundancy; only four out of the eight cameras are in use at any one time. The cameras use visible light to capture <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopy" title="Stereoscopy">stereoscopic</a> three-dimensional (3-D) imagery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HazardAvoidanceCameras_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-HazardAvoidanceCameras-89">[89]</a></sup> The cameras have a 120° <a href="/wiki/Field_of_view" title="Field of view">field of view</a> and map the terrain at up to 3 m (9.8 ft) in front of the rover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HazardAvoidanceCameras_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-HazardAvoidanceCameras-89">[89]</a></sup> This imagery safeguards against the rover crashing into unexpected obstacles, and works in tandem with software that allows the rover to make its own safety choices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HazardAvoidanceCameras_89-4"><a href="#cite_note-HazardAvoidanceCameras-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Mars_Hand_Lens_Imager_.28MAHLI.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mars_Hand_Lens_Imager_(MAHLI)">Mars Hand Lens Imager (MAHLI)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mars Hand Lens Imager (MAHLI)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:169px;max-width:169px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:125px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-MAHLI.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="688" data-file-width="917" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-MAHLI.jpg/167px-PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-MAHLI.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-MAHLI.jpg/251px-PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-MAHLI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-MAHLI.jpg/334px-PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-MAHLI.jpg 2x" width="167"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="#Mars_Hand_Lens_Imager_(MAHLI)">Mars Hand Lens Imager</a> (MAHLI)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:169px;max-width:169px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:125px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA16160-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-APXS.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="738" data-file-width="983" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/PIA16160-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-APXS.jpg/167px-PIA16160-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-APXS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/PIA16160-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-APXS.jpg/251px-PIA16160-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-APXS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/PIA16160-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-APXS.jpg/334px-PIA16160-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-APXS.jpg 2x" width="167"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="#Alpha_Particle_X-ray_Spectrometer_(APXS)">Alpha Particle X-Ray Spectrometer</a> (APXS)</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mars_Hand_Lens_Imager" title="Mars Hand Lens Imager">Mars Hand Lens Imager</a></div>
<p>MAHLI is a camera on the rover's robotic arm, and acquires microscopic images of rock and soil. MAHLI can take <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/True-color" title="True-color">true-color</a> images at 1600×1200 <a href="/wiki/Pixel" title="Pixel">pixels</a> with a resolution as high as 14.5 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometers</a> per pixel. MAHLI has an 18.3 to 21.3 mm (0.72 to 0.84 in) focal length and a 33.8–38.5° field of view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAHLI_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-MAHLI-63">[63]</a></sup> MAHLI has both white and ultraviolet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED" title="LED">LED</a> illumination for imaging in darkness or <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescence</a> imaging. MAHLI also has mechanical focusing in a range from infinite to millimetre distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MAHLI_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-MAHLI-63">[63]</a></sup> This system can make some images with <a href="/wiki/Focus_stacking" title="Focus stacking">focus stacking</a> processing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mars_Hand_Lens_Imager_(MAHLI)_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_Hand_Lens_Imager_(MAHLI)-90">[90]</a></sup> MAHLI can store either the raw images or do real time lossless predictive or JPEG compression. The calibration target for MAHLI includes color references, a metric bar graphic, a 1909 VDB Lincoln penny, and a stairstep pattern for depth calibration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20120913_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20120913-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Alpha_Particle_X-ray_Spectrometer_.28APXS.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Alpha_Particle_X-ray_Spectrometer_(APXS)">Alpha Particle X-ray Spectrometer (APXS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Alpha Particle X-ray Spectrometer (APXS)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle_X-ray_spectrometer" title="Alpha particle X-ray spectrometer">Alpha particle X-ray spectrometer</a></div>
<p>The device irradiates samples with <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> and maps the spectra of <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> that are re-emitted for determining the elemental composition of samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLAPXS_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSLAPXS-92">[92]</a></sup> <i>Curiosity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> APXS was developed by the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency" title="Canadian Space Agency">Canadian Space Agency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLAPXS_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-MSLAPXS-92">[92]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MacDonald_Dettwiler" title="MacDonald Dettwiler">MacDonald Dettwiler (MDA)</a>, the Canadian aerospace company that built the <a href="/wiki/Canadarm" title="Canadarm">Canadarm</a> and <a href="/wiki/RADARSAT" title="RADARSAT">RADARSAT</a>, were responsible for the engineering design and building of the APXS. The APXS science team includes members from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Guelph" title="University of Guelph">University of Guelph</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_New_Brunswick" title="University of New Brunswick">University of New Brunswick</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Western_Ontario" title="University of Western Ontario">University of Western Ontario</a>, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_San_Diego" title="University of California, San Diego">University of California, San Diego</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usra_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-usra-93">[93]</a></sup> The APXS instrument takes advantage of <a href="/wiki/Particle-induced_X-ray_emission" title="Particle-induced X-ray emission">particle-induced X-ray emission</a> (PIXE) and <a href="/wiki/X-ray_fluorescence" title="X-ray fluorescence">X-ray fluorescence</a>, previously exploited by the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Pathfinder" title="Mars Pathfinder">Mars Pathfinder</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Exploration_Rover" title="Mars Exploration Rover">Mars Exploration Rovers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSLAPXS_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-MSLAPXS-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_new_Athena_alpha_particle_X-ray_spectrometer_for_the_Mars_Exploration_Rovers_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_new_Athena_alpha_particle_X-ray_spectrometer_for_the_Mars_Exploration_Rovers-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Chemistry_and_Mineralogy_.28CheMin.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry_and_Mineralogy_(CheMin)">Chemistry and Mineralogy (CheMin)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Chemistry and Mineralogy (CheMin)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:169px;max-width:169px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:124px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Closed.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1181" data-file-width="1581" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Closed.jpg/167px-PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Closed.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Closed.jpg/251px-PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Closed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Closed.jpg/334px-PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Closed.jpg 2x" width="167"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:169px;max-width:169px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:124px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Open.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1181" data-file-width="1581" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Open.jpg/167px-PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Open.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Open.jpg/251px-PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Open.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Open.jpg/334px-PIA16161-Mars_Curiosity_Rover-CheMin-Open.jpg 2x" width="167"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><i>Curiosity</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> <a href="#Chemistry_and_Mineralogy_(CheMin)">CheMin Spectrometer</a> on Mars (September 11, 2012), with sample inlet seen closed and open.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA16217-MarsCuriosityRover-1stXRayView-20121017.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="582" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/PIA16217-MarsCuriosityRover-1stXRayView-20121017.jpg/220px-PIA16217-MarsCuriosityRover-1stXRayView-20121017.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/PIA16217-MarsCuriosityRover-1stXRayView-20121017.jpg/330px-PIA16217-MarsCuriosityRover-1stXRayView-20121017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/PIA16217-MarsCuriosityRover-1stXRayView-20121017.jpg/440px-PIA16217-MarsCuriosityRover-1stXRayView-20121017.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA16217-MarsCuriosityRover-1stXRayView-20121017.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography#Mineralogy_and_metallurgy" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray diffraction</a> view of <a href="/wiki/Martian_soil" title="Martian soil">Martian soil</a> (<i>Curiosity</i> at <a href="/wiki/Rocknest_(Mars)" title="Rocknest (Mars)">Rocknest</a>, October 17, 2012).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20121030_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20121030-95">[95]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/CheMin" title="CheMin">CheMin</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/CheMin" title="CheMin">CheMin</a> is the Chemistry and Mineralogy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray</a> <a href="/wiki/Powder_diffraction" title="Powder diffraction">powder diffraction</a> and <a href="/wiki/X-ray_fluorescence" title="X-ray fluorescence">fluorescence</a> instrument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSL_CheMin_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSL_CheMin-96">[96]</a></sup> CheMin is one of four <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectrometers</a>. It can identify and quantify the abundance of the minerals on Mars. It was developed by David Blake at NASA <a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">Ames Research Center</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fluorescence_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-fluorescence-97">[97]</a></sup> and won the 2013 NASA Government Invention of the year award.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20140624_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20140624-98">[98]</a></sup> The rover can drill samples from rocks and the resulting fine powder is poured into the instrument via a sample inlet tube on the top of the vehicle. A beam of X-rays is then directed at the powder and the crystal structure of the minerals deflects it at characteristic angles, allowing scientists to identify the minerals being analyzed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ski_package_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-ski_package-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>On October 17, 2012, at "<a href="/wiki/Rocknest_(Mars)" title="Rocknest (Mars)">Rocknest</a>", the first <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography#Mineralogy_and_metallurgy" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray diffraction analysis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Martian_soil" title="Martian soil">Martian soil</a> was performed. The results revealed the presence of several minerals, including <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">feldspar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a>, and suggested that the Martian soil in the sample was similar to the "weathered <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basaltic soils</a>" of <a href="/wiki/Hawai%CA%BBi_Volcanoes_National_Park" title="Hawaiʻi Volcanoes National Park">Hawaiian volcanoes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20121030_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20121030-95">[95]</a></sup> The paragonetic <a href="/wiki/Tephra" title="Tephra">tephra</a> from a Hawaiian <a href="/wiki/Cinder_cone" title="Cinder cone">cinder cone</a> has been mined to create <a href="/wiki/Martian_regolith_simulant" title="Martian regolith simulant">Martian regolith simulant</a> for researchers to use since 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beegle2007_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beegle2007-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen1997_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen1997-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Sample_Analysis_at_Mars_.28SAM.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_Analysis_at_Mars_(SAM)">Sample Analysis at Mars (SAM)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Curiosity_(rover)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sample Analysis at Mars (SAM)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="tsingle"
